Islam in Lebanon">Shia</a>,<sup><a href="#3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a>,<sup><a href="#3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon#Alawites" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Alawites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon#Ismailis" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Ismailis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Muslims" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive Muslims">progressive Muslims</a><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a>)<sup><a href="#4">4</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Lebanon" title="Christianity in Lebanon">Christianity</a> (<b>40.5% in Lebanon; majority of diaspora</b>):<sup><a href="#1">1</a></sup><br />(<a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melkite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Melkite Christianity in Lebanon">Melkite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestantism in Lebanon">Protestant</a>)</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">Related ethnic groups</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Other <a href="/wiki/Semitic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Semitic language">Semitic-speaking</a> peoples</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;padding-top:0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:left;"><hr />Notes:
<ol><li>Lebanese Christians of all denominations constitute the <a href="/wiki/Majority" title="Majority">majority</a> of all Lebanese worldwide, but represent only a large <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">minority</a> within Lebanon.</li>
<li>Lebanese Muslims of all denominations represent a majority within Lebanon, but add up to only a large <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">minority</a> of all Lebanese worldwide.</li>
<li>Shias and Sunnis account for 54% of Lebanon's population together, even split in half (27%).</li>
<li>In Lebanon, the Druze quasi-Muslim sect is officially categorized as a Muslim denomination by the Lebanese government.</li></ol>

<p>The <b>Lebanese people</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">الشعب اللبناني</span>&#8206; / <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">ALA-LC</a>: <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration">ash-shaʻb al-Lubnānī</i></i>, <small>Levantine Arabic pronunciation:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[eʃˈʃaʕeb ellɪbˈneːne]</a></span>) are the people inhabiting or originating from <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>. The term may also include those who had inhabited <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Lebanon_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Lebanon mountains">Anti-Lebanon mountains</a> prior to the creation of the modern Lebanese state. The major religious groups among the Lebanese people within Lebanon are <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Shia_Muslims)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Shia Muslims)">Shia Muslims</a> (27%), <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Sunni_Muslims)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Sunni Muslims)">Sunni Muslims</a> (27%), <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Maronite_Christians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Maronite Christians)">Maronite Christians</a> (21%), <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Greek_Orthodox_Christians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Greek Orthodox Christians)">Greek Orthodox Christians</a> (8%), <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Melkite_Christians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Melkite Christians)">Melkite Christians</a> (5%), <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Druze_followers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Druze followers)">Druze</a> (5.6%), <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people_(Protestant_Christians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese people (Protestant Christians)">Protestant Christians</a> (1%).<sup id="cite_ref-2012Report_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Report-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The largest contingente of Lebanese, however, comprise a <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">diaspora</a> in North America, South America, Europe, Australia and Africa, which is predominantly Maronite Christian.
</p><p>As the relative proportion of the various sects is politically sensitive, Lebanon has not collected official <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">census</a> data on ethnic background since the 1932 under the <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon">French Mandate</a>. It is therefore difficult to have an exact demographic analysis of Lebanese society.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
The largest concentration of people of <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">Lebanese ancestry</a> may be in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> having an estimated population of 5.8 to 7 million, but it may be an exaggeration, given that an official survey conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Institute_of_Geography_and_Statistics" title="Brazilian Institute of Geography and Statistics">Brazilian Institute of Geography and Statistics</a> (IBGE) showed that less than 1 million Brazilians claimed any Middle-Eastern origin.<sup id="cite_ref-IBGE2008_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBGE2008-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Lebanese have always traveled the world, many of them settling permanently, most notably in the last two centuries.
</p><p>Estimated to have lost their status as the majority in Lebanon itself, with their reduction in numbers largely as a result of their <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a>, Christians still remain one of the principal religious groups in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-cnewacanada1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnewacanada1-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Descendants of Lebanese <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Lebanon" title="Christianity in Lebanon">Christians</a> make up the majority of Lebanese people worldwide, appearing principally in <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">the diaspora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationalism" title="Lebanese nationalism">Lebanese nationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lebanon" title="History of Lebanon">History of Lebanon</a></div>
<p>Immediately prior to the introduction of Arabic, the people residing in Lebanon—both those who would become Muslim and the vast majority who would remain Christian, along with the tiny <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jewish</a> minority—spoke Aramaic,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> or more precisely, a <a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic_languages" title="Western Aramaic languages">Western Aramaic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Owens_2000_347_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owens_2000_347-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> However, since at least the 15th century, the majority of people of all faiths living in what is now Lebanon have been <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Arabic" title="Lebanese Arabic">Arabic-speaking</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> or more specifically, speakers of <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Arabic" title="Lebanese Arabic">Lebanese Arabic</a>, although up until the 17th century, travellers in the Lebanon still reported on several Aramaic-speaking villages.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the Lebanese <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronites</a>, Aramaic still remains the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical language">liturgical language</a> of the Maronite Church, although in an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">Eastern Aramaic</a> form (the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> in which early Christianity was disseminated throughout the Middle East), distinct from the spoken Aramaic of Lebanon, which was a <a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic_languages" title="Western Aramaic languages">Western Aramaic language</a>. As the second of two liturgical languages of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, Aramaic was also retained as a language in the sphere of religion (in the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>) among <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese Jews">Lebanese Jews</a>, although here too in an Eastern Aramaic form (the Talmud was composed in Babylonia in Babylonian Aramaic). Among Lebanese Muslims, however, Aramaic was lost twice, once in the shift to Arabic in the vernacular (Lebanese Arabic) and again in the religious sphere, since Arabic (Qur'anic Arabic) is the liturgical language of Islam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Some Lebanese Christians, particularly Maronites, identify themselves as Lebanese rather than Arab, seeking to draw "on the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> past to try to forge an identity separate from the prevailing <a href="/wiki/Arab_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab culture">Arab culture</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-reuters1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters1-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> They argue that Arabization merely represented a shift to the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> of the Lebanese people, and that, according to them, no actual shift of <a href="/wiki/Ethnicity" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnicity">ethnic</a> identity, much less ancestral origins, occurred. with their own histories and lore, and that therefore they do not belong to the one pan-Arab <a href="/wiki/Ethnicity" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnicity">ethnicity</a>, and thus such categorisation is erred or inapplicable. Certain portions of Lebanon's Christian population in particular tend to stress aspects of Lebanon's non-<a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> prior history to encompass all Lebanon's historical stages, instead of considering the beginning of Lebanese history being with the Arab conquests.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In light of this "old controversy about identity",<sup id="cite_ref-reuters1_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters1-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> some Lebanese prefer to see Lebanon, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Lebanon" title="Culture of Lebanon">Lebanese culture</a> and themselves as part of "<a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_race" title="Mediterranean race">Mediterranean</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levantine</a>" civilization, in a concession to Lebanon's various layers of heritage, both indigenous, foreign non-Arab, and Arab.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_numbers">Population numbers</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Lebanon" title="Demographics of Lebanon">Demographics of Lebanon</a></div>
<p>The total Lebanese population is estimated at 13 to 18 million. Of these, the vast majority, or 8.6<sup id="cite_ref-dailystar.com.lb_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailystar.com.lb-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> - 14<sup id="cite_ref-fco_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> million, constitute part of the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">Lebanese diaspora</a> (residing outside of Lebanon), with approximately 4.7 million residing in Lebanon itself.<sup id="cite_ref-2012Report_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Report-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lebanon">Lebanon</span></h3>
<div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;">
<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">
<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cia_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cia_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">

</tr>

</tbody></table>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are approximately 4.7 million Lebanese citizens in Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-2012Report_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Report-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to this figure, there are an additional 1 million <a href="/wiki/Foreign_workers" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign workers">foreign workers</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Syrians_in_Lebanon" title="Syrians in Lebanon">Syrians</a>), and about 470,000 <a href="/wiki/Palestinians_in_Lebanon" title="Palestinians in Lebanon">Palestinian</a> <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lebanon is also a home to various ethnic minorities found refuge in the country over the centuries. Prominent ethnic minorities in the country include the <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Lebanon" title="Armenians in Lebanon">Armenians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kurds_in_Lebanon" title="Kurds in Lebanon">Kurds</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Turks_in_Lebanon" title="Turks in Lebanon">Turks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Lebanon" title="Assyrians in Lebanon">Assyrians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iranians_in_Lebanon" title="Iranians in Lebanon">Iranians</a> and many European ethnicities (<a href="/wiki/Greeks_in_Lebanon" title="Greeks in Lebanon">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Lebanese" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian Lebanese">Italians</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Lebanese" class="mw-redirect" title="French Lebanese">French</a>).
</p><p>There are also a small number of nomadic Dom Gypsies<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (part of the Roma people of South Asian, particularly, Indian descent)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diaspora">Diaspora</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">Lebanese diaspora</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rima_Fakih.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Rima_Fakih.jpg/110px-Rima_Fakih.jpg" decoding="async" width="110" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Rima_Fakih.jpg/165px-Rima_Fakih.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Rima_Fakih.jpg/220px-Rima_Fakih.jpg 2x" data-file-width="256" data-file-height="413" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rima_Fakih.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rima_Fakih" title="Rima Fakih">Rima Fakih</a>, winner of <a href="/wiki/Miss_USA_2010" title="Miss USA 2010">Miss USA 2010</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg/110px-Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg" decoding="async" width="110" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg/165px-Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg/220px-Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1409" data-file-height="2008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlos_Ghosn_-_India_Economic_Summit_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carlos_Ghosn" title="Carlos Ghosn">Carlos Ghosn</a></div></div></div> 
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg/110px-Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg" decoding="async" width="110" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg/165px-Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg/220px-Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg 2x" data-file-width="531" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amal_Alamuddin_Cannes_2016.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Amal_Clooney" title="Amal Clooney">Amal Clooney</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">Lebanese diaspora</a> consists of approximately 8.6<sup id="cite_ref-dailystar.com.lb_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailystar.com.lb-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> - 14<sup id="cite_ref-fco_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> million, both Lebanese-born living abroad and those born-abroad of Lebanese descent. The majority of the Lebanese in the diaspora are Christians,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> disproportionately so in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> where the vast majority reside. An estimate figure show that they represent about 75% of the Lebanese in total. Lebanese abroad are considered "rich, educated and influential"<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and over the course of time immigration has yielded Lebanese "commercial networks" throughout the world.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest number of Lebanese is to be found in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, where according to the Brazilian and Lebanese governments claim, there are 7 million Brazilians of Lebanese descent.<sup id="cite_ref-itamaraty.gov.br_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itamaraty.gov.br-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-libano1_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libano1-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Daily_Star_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Daily_Star-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> These figures, however, may be an exaggeration given that, according to a 2008 survey conducted by <a href="/wiki/IBGE" class="mw-redirect" title="IBGE">IBGE</a>, in 2008, covering only the states of Amazonas, Paraíba, São Paulo, Rio Grande do Sul, Mato Grosso and Distrito Federal, 0.9% of white Brazilian respondents said they had family origins in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a><sup id="cite_ref-IBGE2008_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBGE2008-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Large numbers also reside elsewhere in North America, most notably in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (489,702)<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the people of full or partial Lebanese descent are between 190,275 (by ancestry, <a href="/wiki/Canada_2011_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada 2011 Census">2011 Census</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> to 250,000 based on estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-Canada_and_Lebanon,_a_special_tie_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_and_Lebanon,_a_special_tie-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the rest of the Americas, significant communities are found in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-lparg_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lparg-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> (400,000);<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Arab_Chileans_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arab_Chileans-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a><sup id="cite_ref-anba.com.br_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anba.com.br-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, with almost every other <a href="/wiki/Latin_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin American">Latin American</a> country having at least a small presence.
</p><p>In Africa, <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> are home to over 100,000 Lebanese.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> There are significant Lebanese populations in other countries throughout <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> hosts over 180,000 and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> 250,000. In the Arab world, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Arab states of the Persian Gulf</a> harbour around 400,000 Lebanese.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Lebanese people also can be found in all of the 28 <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Member states of the European Union">member states of the European Union</a>. More than 2,500 ex-<a href="/wiki/South_Lebanon_Army" title="South Lebanon Army">SLA</a> members remain in Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently, Lebanon provides no automatic right to <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a> for emigrants who lost their citizenship upon acquiring the citizenship of their host country, nor for the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigrants</a> born abroad. This situation disproportionately affects Christians. Recently, the Maronite Institution of Emigrants called for the establishment of an avenue by which emigrants who lost their citizenship may regain it, or their overseas-born descendants (if they so wish) may acquire it.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The list below contains approximate figures for people of Lebanese descent by country of residence, largely taken from the iLoubnan diaspora map.<sup id="cite_ref-iloub_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iloub-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Additional reliable cites have been provided where possible. Additional estimates have been included where they can be cited; where applicable, these are used in place of the iLoubnan figures. The Figure below uses the data from the list and calculates the amount of Lebanese residents as a percentage of the total population of the respective country.
</p>

<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top:1px solid #aaa;">Language</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>

<dd><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>

<dd><a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a></dd></dl></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top:1px solid #aaa;">Politics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Lebanon" title="Politics of Lebanon">Lebanese politics</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of Lebanon">President</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Prime Ministers of Lebanon">Prime Minister</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Lebanon" title="List of political parties in Lebanon">List of political parties in Lebanon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Pact" title="National Pact">National Pact</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationalism" title="Lebanese nationalism">Lebanese nationalism</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phoenicianism" title="Phoenicianism">Phoenicianism</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Lebanon" title="Coat of arms of Lebanon">Coat of arms of Lebanon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Lebanon" title="Flag of Lebanon">Flag of Lebanon</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Lebanon.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of Lebanon.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Flag_of_Lebanon.svg/16px-Flag_of_Lebanon.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="11" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Flag_of_Lebanon.svg/24px-Flag_of_Lebanon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Flag_of_Lebanon.svg/32px-Flag_of_Lebanon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Lebanon" title="Portal:Lebanon">Lebanon&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Lebanese_people" title="Template:Lebanese people"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Lebanese_people" title="Template talk:Lebanese people"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Lebanese_people&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> has several different main <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a>. The country has the most religiously diverse society in the Middle East, encompassing 17 recognized religious sects.<sup id="cite_ref-libcon_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libcon-100">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The main two religions among the Lebanese people are <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Lebanon" title="Christianity in Lebanon">Christianity</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Church_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Church in Lebanon">Maronite Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Church in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Melkite_Church_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Melkite Church in Lebanon">Melkite</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Church_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Church in Lebanon">Protestant Church</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Lebanon" title="Islam in Lebanon">Islam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shia_branch_of_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia branch of Islam in Lebanon">Shia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunni_branch_of_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni branch of Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a>). There is also the <a href="/wiki/Druzites_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druzites in Lebanon">Druze</a> quasi-Muslim sect.
</p><p>There are other <a href="/wiki/Minorities_(Lebanon)" title="Minorities (Lebanon)">non-Lebanese Christian minorities</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Lebanon" title="Armenians in Lebanon">Armenians</a> (<a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Catholic_Church" title="Armenian Catholic Church">Armenian Catholic Church</a>), <a href="/wiki/French_people_in_Lebanon" title="French people in Lebanon">French</a>-<a href="/wiki/Italians_in_Lebanon" title="Italians in Lebanon">Italians</a> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_Church_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin Church in Lebanon">Latin Catholic Church</a>), <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Lebanon" title="Assyrians in Lebanon">Assyrians</a> (<a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">Syriac Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic Church</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a> (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>), who immigrated to Lebanon over the years.<sup id="cite_ref-dl_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dl-101">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>No official census has been taken since 1932, reflecting the political sensitivity in Lebanon over confessional (i.e. religious) balance.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study conducted by Statistics Lebanon, a Beirut-based research firm, cited by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a> found that of Lebanon's population of approximately 4.3 million is estimated to be:<sup id="cite_ref-2012Report_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Report-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>54%</b> <b><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Lebanon" title="Islam in Lebanon">Islam</a></b> (<a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Shia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a>, 27% each)</li>
<li><b>40.5%</b> <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Lebanon" title="Christianity in Lebanon"><b>Christian</b></a> (21% <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite</a>, 8% <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a>, 5% <a href="/wiki/Melkite_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Melkite Catholics">Melkite Catholics</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestantism in Lebanon">Protestant</a>, 5.5% <a href="/wiki/Minorities_(Lebanon)" title="Minorities (Lebanon)">other minority Christian denominations</a> like <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Rite Catholics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Catholic_Church" title="Armenian Catholic Church">Armenian Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">Syriac Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">Chaldean Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Catholic_Church_of_the_East" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Catholic Church of the East">Assyrian Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox</a>)</li>
<li><b>5.5%</b> <b><a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a></b> (a quasi-Muslim sect who do not consider themselves to be Muslim, even though under the terms of the Lebanese Constitution the Druze community is designated as a part of the Lebanese Muslim community.)</li></ul>
<p>There are also very small numbers of other religions such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Lebanon" title="History of the Jews in Lebanon">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mormons" title="Mormons">Mormons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá&#39;í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>, and also religions practiced by foreigner workers like <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2012Report_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012Report-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a> specifies that of those residing in Lebanon, 59.7% are <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Lebanon" title="Islam in Lebanon">Muslims</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Shia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alawites" title="Alawites">Alawites</a>) and 39% are <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Lebanon" title="Christianity in Lebanon">Christians</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melkite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Melkite Christianity in Lebanon">Melkite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Catholic_Church" title="Armenian Catholic Church">Armenian Catholic Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaldean Catholic">Chaldean Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">Syriac Catholics</a>) and 1.3% "Other".<sup id="cite_ref-cia_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Foundation_for_Electoral_Systems" title="International Foundation for Electoral Systems">International Foundation for Electoral Systems</a> provides source for the registered voters in Lebanon for 2011<sup id="cite_ref-2011data_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2011data-103">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> (it has to be noted that voter registration does not include people under 18 and unregistered voters) that puts the numbers as following: 
<a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni Islam</a> 27.65%, <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Shia Islam</a> 27.34%,
<a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite Catholic</a> 21.05%, <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a> 7.34%, <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a> 5.74%, <a href="/wiki/Melkite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Melkite Christianity in Lebanon">Melkite Catholic</a> 4.76%, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic</a> 2.64%, <a href="/wiki/Minorities_(Lebanon)" title="Minorities (Lebanon)">other Christian Minorities</a> 1.28%, <a href="/wiki/Alawites_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Alawites in Lebanon">Alawite Shia Islam</a> 0.88%, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian Catholic">Armenian Catholic</a> 0.62%, <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestantism in Lebanon">Evangelical Protestant</a> 0.53%, and other 0.18% of the population.
</p><p>However, as soon as the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_diaspora" title="Lebanese diaspora">diaspora</a> is included, the Christians become an absolute majority. Lebanon has a population of <a href="/wiki/Mhallami" title="Mhallami">Mhallamis</a> also known as <i>Mardinli</i>), most of whom migrated from northeast <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and southeast <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> are estimated to be between 75,000 and 100,000 and considered to be part of the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a> population. These have in recent years been granted <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a> and, coupled with several <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">civil wars</a> between Islamic extremists and the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Armed_Forces" title="Lebanese Armed Forces">Lebanese military</a> that have caused many Christians to flee the country, have re-tipped the demographic balance in favour of the Muslims and the Sunnis in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In addition, many thousands of Arab <a href="/wiki/Bedouin_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bedouin people">Bedouins</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_Valley" title="Beqaa Valley">Bekaa</a> and in the Wadi Khaled region, who are entirely Sunnis, were granted <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a>. Lebanon also has a <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Lebanon" title="History of the Jews in Lebanon">Jewish</a> population, estimated at less than 100.
</p><p>Even though Lebanon is a secular country, family matters such as marriage, divorce and inheritance are still handled by the religious authorities representing a person's faith. Calls for civil marriage are unanimously rejected by the religious authorities but civil marriages held in another country are recognized by Lebanese civil authorities.
</p><p>Legally registered <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Lebanon" title="Islam in Lebanon">Muslims</a> form around 54% of the population (Shia, Sunni, Alawite). Legally registered <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Lebanon" title="Christianity in Lebanon">Christians</a> form up to 41% (Maronite, Greek Orthodox Christian, Melkite, Armenian, Evangelical, other). <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a> form around 5%. A small minority of 0.1% includes <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese Jews">Jews</a>, and foreign workers who belong to <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> religions.
</p><p>Even though non-religion is not recognized by the state, in 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Interior_and_Municipalities_(Lebanon)" title="Ministry of Interior and Municipalities (Lebanon)">Minister of the Interior</a> <a href="/wiki/Ziad_Baroud" class="mw-redirect" title="Ziad Baroud">Ziad Baroud</a> made it possible to have the religious sect removed from the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_identity_card" title="Lebanese identity card">Lebanese identity card</a>, this does not, however, deny the religious authorities complete control over civil family issues inside the country.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg/220px-Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg/330px-Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d4/Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tristram_Ellis_002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Druze family of the Lebanon, late 1800s</div></div></div>
<p>According to a study published by the <a href="/wiki/American_Journal_of_Human_Genetics" title="American Journal of Human Genetics">American Journal of Human Genetics</a>, present-day Lebanese derive most of their ancestry from a <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaanite-related population</a>, which therefore implies substantial genetic continuity in the Levant since at least the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> More specifically, according to Chris Tyler-Smith, a geneticist and his colleagues at the <a href="/wiki/Sanger_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanger Institute">Sanger Institute</a> in Britain, who compared "sampled ancient DNA from five <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Canaanite people">Canaanite people</a> who lived 3,750 and 3,650 years ago" to modern people. "The comparison revealed that 90 percent of the genetic ancestry of people in Lebanon came from the <a href="/wiki/Canaanites" class="mw-redirect" title="Canaanites">Canaanites</a>. (The other 10 percent was of a <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_steppe" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian steppe">Eurasian steppe</a> population.)"<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In recent years efforts have been made by various <a href="/wiki/Genetic_genealogy" title="Genetic genealogy">genetic researchers</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2011)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> both in Lebanon and abroad, to identify the ancestral origins of the Lebanese people, their relationship to each other, and to other neighbouring and distant human populations. Like most <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> studies that attempt to identify a population's origins and migration patterns in the region that may have influenced the genetic make-up—these studies have focused on two <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> segments, the <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a> (inherited only by males and passed only by fathers) and <a href="/wiki/MtDNA" class="mw-redirect" title="MtDNA">mtDNA</a> (mitochondrial DNA, which passes only from mother to child). Both segments are unaffected by recombination, thus they provide an indicator of paternal and maternal origins, respectively.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Theories from some studies propose to corroborate that the Lebanese trace genetic continuity with earlier inhabitants, regardless of their membership to any of Lebanon's different religious communities today. "The genetic marker which identifies descendants of the ancient Levantines is found among members of all of Lebanon's religious communities"<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> as well as some Syrians and Palestinians. By identifying the ancient type of DNA attributed to the Phoenicians, geneticist Pierre Zalloua was also able to chart their spread out of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>. These markers were found in unusually high proportions in non-Lebanese samples from other parts of the "Mediterranean coast where the Phoenicians are known to have established colonies, such as <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> in today's <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-reuters1_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters1-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The markers were also found among samples of <a href="/wiki/Maltese_people" title="Maltese people">Maltese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spaniards" title="Spaniards">Spaniards</a>, where the Phoenicians were also known to have established colonies. The study shows that 1 out of 17 people in the countries surrounding the Mediterranean basin can be identified with the Phoenician genetic markers in their male chromosomes. However, the particular marker associated by some studies with the historical Phoenicians, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J2" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup J2">haplogroup J2</a>, actually represents a complex mosaic of different demographic processes which affected the Mediterranean in prehistoric and historic times.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond this, more recent finds have also interested geneticists and Lebanese <a href="/wiki/Anthropologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropologists">anthropologists</a>. These indicate foreign non-Levantine admixture from some unexpected but not surprising sources, even if only in a small proportion of the samples. Like a story written in DNA, it recounts some of the major historical events seen in the land today known as Lebanon.
</p><p>Among the more interesting genetic markers found are those that seem to indicate that a small proportion of Lebanese Christians (2%) and a smaller proportion of Lebanese Muslims are descended, in part, from European <a href="/wiki/Crusaders" class="mw-redirect" title="Crusaders">Crusader Christians</a> and Arabian Muslims respectively. The author states that the "study tells us that some European crusaders did not just conquer and leave behind castles. They left a subtle genetic connection as well."<sup id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-112">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In much the same manner, some of the Arabian Muslims did not just conquer and leave behind mosques.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon,_late_1800s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon%2C_late_1800s.jpg/220px-Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon%2C_late_1800s.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon%2C_late_1800s.jpg/330px-Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon%2C_late_1800s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon%2C_late_1800s.jpg/440px-Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon%2C_late_1800s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1441" data-file-height="888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lebanese_Christian_men_from_Mount_Lebanon,_late_1800s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christian men from <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a>, late 1800s</div></div></div>
<p>It was during a broader survey of <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle Eastern</a> populations conducted for the <a href="/wiki/Genographic_Project" title="Genographic Project">Genographic Project</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a> that the findings were stumbled upon. "We noticed some interesting lineages in the dataset. Among Lebanese Christians, in particular, we found higher frequency (2%) of a genetic marker — <a href="/wiki/R1b" class="mw-redirect" title="R1b">R1b</a> — that we typically see only in Western Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-112">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The lineage was seen at that "higher" frequency only in the Christian populations in Lebanon, even though among the Muslims it was not altogether absent. "The study matched the <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western European</a> Y-chromosome lineage against thousands of people in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, in the Lebanese Muslim population a similar pattern, this time associated with genetic markers from <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, was also observed in "higher" preferential frequencies, although they too were not altogether absent in the Christian population. "We found that a lineage that is very common in the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> — Hg J*— is found in slightly higher frequencies preferentially in the Muslim population."<sup id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-112">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
The author of the study added that the findings "certainly doesn't undermine the similarities among the various Lebanese communities, but it does agree with oral tradition."<sup id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-112">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other unrelated studies have sought to establish relationships between the Lebanese people and other groups. At least one study by the International Institute of Anthropology in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, confirmed similarities in the Y-haplotype frequencies in Lebanese, Palestinian, and <a href="/wiki/Sephardim" class="mw-redirect" title="Sephardim">Sephardic Jewish</a> men, identifying them as "three Near-Eastern populations sharing a common geographic origin."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The study surveyed one Y-specific DNA <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphism</a> (p49/Taq I) in 54 Lebanese and 69 Palestinian males, and compared with the results found in 693 <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jews</a> from three distinct Jewish ethnic groups; <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">Mizrahi Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardi Jews</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Ashkenazi Jews</a>.
</p><p>In a 2013 interview <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Zalloua" title="Pierre Zalloua">Pierre Zalloua</a>, pointed out that genetic variation preceded religious variation and divisions: "Lebanon already had well-differentiated communities with their own genetic peculiarities, but not significant differences, and religions came as layers of paint on top. There is no distinct pattern that shows that one community carries significantly more Phoenician than another."<sup id="cite_ref-Maroon_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maroon-115">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Lebanese cluster the closest to Jews of any Arab population except the Druze according to a 2010 study by Behar et al., possibly corroborating a Phoenician origin.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2019 study, carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Wellcome_Sanger_Institute" title="Wellcome Sanger Institute">Wellcome Sanger Institute</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, after analyzing the "DNA evidence from the remains of nine <a href="/wiki/Crusaders" class="mw-redirect" title="Crusaders">Crusaders</a> found at a burial site in Lebanon", concludes that, contrarily to some idea, the Crusaders didn't leave "a lasting effect on the genetics of modern-day Lebanese. Instead, today’s Lebanese Christians in particular are more genetically similar to locals from the Roman period, which preceded the Crusades by more than four centuries."<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
